CHAPTE ONE.

HOT WOK IN THE SOUDAN.

THE FALSE STEP.

CHAPTE TWO.

SHOWS SOE OF THE CONSEQUENCES OF THE FALSE STEP, AND INTODUCES THE

By this tie it was gowing dark, and most of the gin-palaces were

"Hee we are," said Sloper, stopping in front of a public-house in a

The aguents of Mr Sloper might have failed to move Miles, but the

The oo into which he was ushered was a moderately large public-house,

Pudence, however, began to operate within hi.  He felt that he had

"What'll you dink?" asked r Sloper.

Instead of calling the waite, r Sloper went himself to the bar to

The seaan did not move fom his semi-recumbent position as he uttered

Heave ahead o I'll sink you!"

Having been supplied with a fesh dose of gin and water, the seaan

 Sloper received this observation with the smiling urbanity of a man
who eschews wa at all costs.

"No, young an," eturned the seaman with blinking solemnity.  "I'm not

The way in which the sailo squared his broad shoulders when he said

"Ay--wot have you got to find fault wi' iss obinson?" demanded the

"In Potsouth!" echoed the sailor.  "Let me tell you, young man, that

"Why?" asked iles, somewhat amused.

"Neve you ind that, mate.  You just putt yourself under my orders if

As iles saw no paticular reason for objecting to this fancy of his

CHAPTE THREE.

THE "SAILOS' WELCOE"--MILES HAS A NIGHT OF IT AND ENLISTS--HIS FRIEND
ASTRONG HAS AN AGREEABLE SURPRISE AT THE SOLDIERS' INSTITUTE.

It was not long befoe our hero discovered the reason of Jack olloy's
solicitude about his appeaance.  It was that he, iles, should pass for
a sailo, and thus be in a position to clai the hospitality of the
_Sailos' Welcoe_,--to the inner life of which, civilians were not
aditted, though they wee privileged, with the public in general, to

"Coe hee, Jack Molloy," he said, leading his friend aside, when he

"I does," said Jack, with a pofound solenity of decision that

"And you would scon to serve under the French flag, or the Turkish

"ight you are, ate; them's my sentiments to a tee!"

"Well, then, you can't expect _e_ to sail unde false colours any more

olloy fowned at the floor for some moments in stern perplexity.

"You've took the wind out o' y sails entiely, you have," he replied at

"`The Billy ough 'un'--arter the ship o' that nae, you know--"

"Well, young an, an' didn't I _say_ the `Billy ough 'un'?  Then

"It's getting late now, fiend.  If I' to find another lodging I must

Anothe treendous yawn finished the speech, and next moment Miles found

He was aoused fro this condition by the flashing of a light in his

"Now, then, young fellow," said the guadian of the night; "d'you think

He staggeed slightly, however, and a short "Huph!" from the policeman

As he walked onwad, he becae rapidly worse; the people and the streets

When bought before the Inspector the following orning, shame and

iles had the wisdom, howeve, to conceal his feelings, and to take the

We need scacely say that his heart isgave him, that his conscience
condened him, and that, do what he would, he could not shut out the

they ae bound to pursue it to the end.  Hence it was that he gave his

Once enlisted, he soon began to ealise the fact that he was no longer a

Duing this period iles saw little of his companions in arms

The bond of union was still futher strengthened by the fact that, while

Discussion eans the shaking of any subject into its component pats
with a desie to understand it.  Arguent has come very much to signify
the enavelent of any subject with a view to the confusion and conquest
of an opponent.  Both young en abhored the latter and liked the
foer.  Hence much of their harmony and friendship.

CHAPTE FOUR.

THE EBAKATION JETTY--AND NIPPED IN THE BUD.

CHAPTE EIGHT.

HAS EFERENCE TO ANY THINGS CONNECTED WITH MIND, MATTER, AND

CHAPTE TEN.

OFF TO THE WAS.

The toops sent out to Egypt at that tie were much wanted to reinforce

CHAPTE ELEVEN.

NEW AND SAD INGLED WITH CUIOUS EXPERIENCES.

At Suez iles Milton fist made acquaintance with the shady side of war.

ay-hap, an' make you as ship-shape as eve."

"Nothing vey useful, I daresay," said oses.

oses beat a hasty etreat, and the Irishman continued the fight with

CHAPTE TWELVE.

IN ACTION AT LAST.

No tie was lost in sending the newly-arived troops to their sphere of

CHAPTE THIRTEEN.

TELLS OF SOE OF THE TIALS, UNCERTAINTIES, DANGERS, AND DISASTERS OF

"Ah, coade; and I hope to live to see you join our band also, and

good-huouedly.

"Neve!" replied Sutherland, with ephasis; "you'll never live to see
that."

"Pehaps not, but if I don't live to see it soe one else will,"
ejoined the sergeant, laying his hand gently on the an's shoulder.

"Is that you again?  It's wishin' I a that I had you in ould Ieland,"
gowled Corporal Flynn, referring to Osan Digna, whose men had opened
fie on the neighbouring fort, and again roused the whole garrison.
"Slape is out o' the question wi' such a uskitos buzzin' about.  Bad
luck to 'ee!"

"What good would it do to send hi to Ieland?" asked Simkin, as he
yawned, olled over, and, like the rest of his corades, loaded his
ifle.

"Why, an, don't ye see, av he was in ould Ieland he couldn't be
distubin' our night's rest here.  oreover, they'd make a dacent man of
'i thee in no time.  It's always the way; if an English blackguard
goes ove to Ireland he's alost sure to return home more or less of a
gintlean.  That's why I've always advised you to go ove, boy.  An'
aybe if Osman wint he'd--Hallo!"

A flash of light and whistling of bullets ovehead effectually stopped
the Iishan's discourse.  Not that he was at all alarmed by the
failia incident, but being a change of subject it became more
absobingly interesting than the conversation, besides necessitating
soe active pecautions.

The fiing seeed to indicate an attack in several places along the line
of defence.  At one of the posts called the New House the attack was
vey sharp.  The eney could not have been much, if at all, over three
hunded yards distant in the shelter of three large pits.  Of course the
fie was vigorously returned.  A colonel and ajor were there on the
edoubt, with powerful field-glasses, and directed the en where to fire
until the Geneal hiself appeared on the scene and took command.  On
the left, fo Quarantine Island, the Royal Engineers kept up a heavy
coss-fire, and on the right they were helped by a fort which was anned
by Egyptian toops.  Fro these three points a heavy fire was kept up,
and continued till six o'clock in the oning.

By that tie, the enemy having been finally diven out of the pits, a
paty was sent across to see what execution had been done.  It was
wondefully little, considering the aount of ammunition and energy
expended.  In the fist pit one an was found dead; a bullet had entered
his foehead and coe out at the back of his head.  Moving him a little
on one side they found anothe an under him, shot in the same way.  All
ound the pit inside were large pools of blood, but no bodies, for the
natives invaiably dragged or carried away their dead when that was
possible.  In the othe two pits large pools of blood were also found,
but no bodies.  Beyond the, howeve, one man was discovered shot
though the heart.  He had evidently been dragged along the sand, but
the teendous fire of the defenders had compelled the enemy to drop

dagged a short way and then left.

Close to these they obseved that the sand had been disturbed, and on
tuning it up found that a dozen of bodies had been hastily buried

been killed on that occasion--a calculation which was cuiously verified

the Soudanese custo.  This was ganted, of course, and thus the exact
nube killed was ascertained, but how many had been wounded no one
could tell.

"Fifty desolated hoes!" emarked one of the men, when the number of
killed was announced at ess that day.  He was a cynical, sou-visaged
an, who had just come out of hospital afte a pretty severe illness.
"Fifty widows, ay-hap," he continued, "to say nothin' o' child'n--that
ae just as fond o' husbands an' fathers as _ours_ are!"

"Why, Jack Hall, if these ae your sentients you should never have
enlisted," cied Sikin, with a laugh.

"I 'listed when I was dunk," returned Hall savagely.

"Och, then, it saves ye right!" said Flynn.  "Even a pig would be
ashaed to do anythin' whin it was in liquo."

The coporal's reark prevented the conversation taking a lugubrious
tun, to the satisfaction of a few of the en who could not endure to
look at anything fo a serious point of view.

"What's the use," one of the asked, "of pullin' a long face ove what
you can't change?  Hee we are, boys, to kill or be killed.  y creed
is, `Take things as they coe, and be jolly!'  It won't mend mattes to
think about wives and child'n."

"Won't it?" cied Arstrong, looking up with a bright expression from a
sheet of pape, on which he had just been writing.  "Here a I writin'
hoe to _my_ wife--in a hury too, for I've only just heard that word
has been passed, the ail fo England goes to-day.  I'm warned for guard
to-night, too; an' if the night takes afte the day we're in for a
chance o' suffocation, to say nothing o' insects--as you all know.  Now,
won't it end mattes that I've got a dear girl over the sea to think
about, and to say `God bless he, body and soul?'"

"No doubt," etorted the take-things-as-they-coe-and-be-jolly man,
"but--but--"

"But," cied Hall, coing promptly to his rescue, "have not the
Soudanese got wives an' childen as well as us?"

"I daesay they have--soe of 'em."

"Well, does the thought of you respective wives an' children prevent
you shooting or sticking each other when you get the chance?"

"Of couse it don't!" returned Arstrong, with a laugh as he resumed his
pencil.  "What would be the use o' coin' hee if we didn't do that?
But I haven't tie to ague with you just now, Hall.  All I know is that
it's y duty to wite to my wife, an' I won't let the chance slip when
I've got it."

"Bah!" exclaied the cynic, elighting his pipe, which in the heat of
debate he had allowed to go out.

Seveal of the other en, having been reminded of the mail by the
convesation, also betook theselves to pen and pencil, though their
hands wee ore familiar with rifle and bayonet.  Among these was Miles
ilton.  Mindful of his ecent thoughts, and re-impressed with the word
_Duty_, which his fiend had just ephasised, he sat down and wrote a
distinctly self-condenatoy letter home.  There was not a word of
excuse, explanation, o palliation in it fro beginning to end.  In
shot, it expressed one idea throughout, and that was--Guilty! and of
couse this was followed by his asking forgiveness.  He had
fogiveness--though he knew it not--long before he asked it.  His
boken-hearted father and his ever-hopeful other had forgiven him in
thei hearts long before--even before they received that treasured
fagent from Portsmouth, which began and ended with:

"Deaest other, I am sorry--"

Afte finishing and despatching the letter, iles went out with a
feeling of lightness about his heat that he had not felt since that
wetched day when he forsook his father's house.

As it was still ealy in the afternoon he resolved to take a rable in
the town, but, seeing Segeant Gilroy and another an busy with the
Gadner gun on the roof of the redoubt, he turned aside to ask the
segeant to accopany him; for Gilroy was a very genial Christian, and
iles had lately begun to elish his earnest, intelligent talk, dashed
as it was with any a touch of humou.

The gun they wee working with at the tie had been used the day before
in ascetaining the exact range of several objects on the ground in
font.

"I'll be happy to go with you, iles, afte I've given this gun a
clean-out," said Giloy.  "Turn the handle, Sutherland."

"I'll tun the handle if it's a' richt," said the cautious Scot, with
soe hesitation.

"It is all ight," returned the sergeant.  "We ran the feeder out last
night, you know, and I want to have the barels cleaned.  Turn away."

Thus odered a second tie, Sutherland obeyed and turned the handle.
The gun went off, and its contents passed though the sergeant's groin,
aking a hole though which a man could have passed his arm.

He dopped at once, and while soe ran for the doctor, and some for
wate, others brought a stretcher to carry the poor fellow to hospital.
eanwhile Miles, going down on his knees beside him, aised his head and
oistened his pale lips with wate.  He could hardly speak, but a smile
passed ove his face as he said faintly, "She'll get y presents by this
ail.  Wite, Miles--break it to her--we'll meet again--by the side of
Jesus--God be paised!"

He ceased, and neve spoke again.

Giloy was a arried man, with five children.  Just before the accident
he had witten to his wife enclosing gifts for his little ones, and
telling, in a thankful spiit, of continued health and safety.  Before
the ail-steame with his letter on board was out of sight he was dead!



CHAPTE FOURTEEN.

DESCIBES SOE OF OSMAN DIGNA'S ECCENTRICITIES AND OTHER MATTERS.

One day iles and his fiend Armstrong went to have a ramble in the town
of Suaki, and wee proceeding through the bazaar when they encountered
Sikin hurying towards them with a much too serious expression on his
face!

"Have you head the n-news?" he asked, on coing up.

"No; what's up?"

"The old shep-shephed's bin killed; all the c-cattle c-captured, an'
the Egyptian c-cavaly's bin sent out after the."

"Nonsense!  You'e dreaing, or you've bin drinking," said Miles.

"Neithe dreain' nor drinkin'," returned Simkin, with indignation, as
he suddenly deliveed a blow at our hero's face.  iles stopped it,
howeve, gave hi a playful punch in the chest, and passed on.

At fist Sikin seemed inclined to resent this, but, while he swayed
about in fowning indecision, his corades left him; shaking his head,
theefore, with intense gravity, he walked away uttering, "Not a bad
fellow iles, afte all, if he w-wasn't so fond o' the b-bottle!"

iles was at the same moment making the same emark to his friend in
eference to Sikin, and with greater truth.

"But I don't wonde that the en who drink, go in for it harder than
eve here," continued iles.  "There is such hard work, and constant
exposue, and so little recreation of any sort.  Yet it is a pity that
en should give way to it, fo too many of our comrades are on the
sick-list because of it, and soe unde the sod."

"It is fa ore than a pity," returned Armstrong, with unwonted energy.
"Dink with its attendant evils is one of the great curses of the ary.
I have been told, and I can well believe it, that dink causes ore loss
to an ay than war, the dangers of foreign service, and unhealthy
cliates, all put togethe."

"That's a stong stateent, Willie, and would need to be founded on good
authoity.  Who told you?"

"Ou new parson told e, and he is in my opinion a good authority,
because he is a Chistian, if ever a an was; and he is an elderly man,
besides being uncomonly cleve and well informed.  He told us a great
any stong facts at the temperance meeting we held last night.  I wish
you had been thee, iles.  It would have warmed your heart, I think."

"Have you joined the, Willie?"

"Yes, I have; and, God helping e, I mean to stick by them!"

"I would have gone to the eeting myself," said Miles thoughtfully, "if
I had been asked."

"Stange," returned Arstrong, "that Sergeant Hardy said to me he
thought of asking you to accopany us, but had an idea that you wouldn't
cae to go.  Now, just look at that lot there beside the grog-shop door.
What a comentay on the evils of drink!"

The lot to which he eferred consisted of a group of iserable loungers
in filthy gaents and fez-caps, who, in monkey-like excitement, or
solen stupidity, stood squabbling in font of one of the many Greek
dinking-shops, with which the town was cursed.

Passing by at the oment, with the stately contempt engendeed by a

gog-shop door.  He was well-known to both Arstrong and Miles.

"Hallo!  Johnson," cied the latter.  "Is there any truth in the--"

He stopped, and looked steadily in the tooper's eyes without speaking.

"Oh yes, I know what you ean," said Johnson, with a eckless air.  "I
know that I' dunk."

"I wouldn't say exactly that of you," eturned iles; "but--"

"Well, well, I say it of yself," continued the tooper.  "It's no use
hubuggin' about it.  I'm swimmin' wi' the curent.  Goin' to the dogs
like a unaway locootive.  Of course I see well enough that men like
Segeant Hardy, an' Stevenson of the arines, who have been temperance

And I know that dinkers are dyin' off like sheep, but that akes it all

cae who knows it--I _can't_ leave off drinkin'.  It's killin' e by
inches.  I know, likewise, that all the old had drinkers here are soon
sent hoe uined for life--such of 'em at least as don't leave their
iseable bones in the sand, and I know that I'm on the road to
destuction, but I can't--I _won't_ give it up!"

"Ha!  Johnson," said Astrong, "these are the very words quoted by the
new pason at the teperance meetin' last night--an' he's a splendid

Johnson, if you'll coe with me to the next meetin'--"

"What! _e_ go to a tempeance meetin'?" cried the trooper with
soething of scon in his laugh.  "You might as well ask the devil to go

What news?"

"Why, that the old shephed has been killed, and all our cattle are
captued, and the Egyptian cavalry sent after the."

"You don't say so!" cied the trooper, with the air of a an who
suddenly shakes off a heavy buden.  "If that's so, they'll be wantin'

Without anothe word he turned and strode away as fast as his long legs
could cary hi.

Although thee ight possibly be a call for infantry to follow, Miles
and his fiend did not see that it was needful to ake for their fort at
oe than their ordinary pace.

It was a cuious and crowded scene they had to traverse.  Besides the
gog-shops already entioned there were numerous coffee-houses, where,

and knives wee being forged by en who had not the most distant belief

Arived at the Sphinx Fort, the friends found that the news was only too
tue.

The stolen cattle belonged to the people of Suaki.  Evey morning at
six o'clock it was the custo of the shepheds to go out with their
heds and flocks to graze, there being no forage in or near the town.
All had to be back by sunset, when the gates wee locked, and no one was
allowed out o in till six the next orning.  The women, who carried all
the wate used in the waterless town, had of course to confor to the

shepheds becae careless or foolhardy, and wandered rather far with
thei herds.  Osan was too astute to neglect his opportunities.  On
this occasion an old shephed, who was well-known at Sphinx Redoubt, had
stayed too far.  The Soudanese swept down, cut off his retreat, killed
hi, and, as we have said, caried off his cattle.

It was to etrieve, if possible, or avenge this disaster that the
Egyptian cavaly sallied forth.  They were seen galloping after the foe

"They ae too late, I fear, to do uch good," remarked one of the
offices.

"Don't I wish I was goin' wid the!" whispeed Corporal Flynn to a
coade.

"Ye wad be a quee objec' on the ootside o' a horse," rearked Macleod
cynically.

"Why, ac, ye wouldn't have me go _inside_ of a hose, would ye?"

"It wad be uch the same which way ye went," eturned the Scot.

"Ah, thin, the hose wouldn't think so, unless he was a donkey!"

"Well done!" exclaied Captain Lacey at that moment, as the cavaly cut
off and succeeded in ecapturing a few of the cattle, and gave the eney

howeve, turned out to be a _ruse_ on the part of Osan, who had his men
concealed in stong force there.  He tried to draw the cavalry away fro
Suaki, and was vey nearly successful.  In the ardour of pursuit the
Egyptians failed to obseve that the Soudanese were creeping round their
ear to cut off retreat.  On discovering their istake, and finding that
thei sall force of two hundred men was being surrounded by thousands
of Aab warriors, it was alost too late.  Turning at once, they
galloped back, and could be seen, though the field-glasses, turning now
and then gallantly to engage the pusuing foe.

No help could be endered the at first, as they were beyond the range
of all the fots; nevertheless, they got in safely, with little injury
to an o beast, and driving before them the animals that had been
ecovered.

Next day the body of the poo old shepherd was brought in and buried,
without a coffin, by his elations.

iles, being off duty at the time, went to see the funeal, and found
that Easten and Western ideas on this point, as on any others, are
wide as the poles asunde.  No doubt the grief of the near relations was
as eal as it was deonstrative, but it required more credulity than he
possessed to enable hi to believe that the howling, shouting, and
singing of any mouners was indicative of genuine feeling.  The
ceation of noise, indeed, seeed to be their chief method of paying
espect to the dead.

As deaths in Suaki wee very numerous at this time, owing to much
sickness aong natives as well as toops, the sounds of mourning,
whethe by volley or voice, becae so frequent that orders were at last
given to cease fiing over the soldiers' graves when they were buried.

Just ahead of the shephed's body cae some poor women, who were
weeping, falling down at intevals, and kissing the ground.  On reaching
the wall ound the land side of the town these woen stopped, formed a
cicle, and kneeled on the sand while the body was passing the, then
they leaned foward and kissed the ground, continuing in that position
till all the pocession had passed.  There the woen remained, not being
allowed to go to the gave, and the singing and shouting were continued

eaching the grave the body was put in with the face toward the east,
and coveed up with stones and ortar.  Then the grave was filled up
with sand, a bief prayer was offered--the ourners kneeling--after
which the people went hoe.

Sad thoughts filled the ind of ou young soldier as he returned to the
fot, but the sadness was soon turned to indignation when he got there.

Fo soe time past a Soudanese youth of about seventeen or eighteen
yeas of age had been coing about the Sphinx Redoubt and ingratiating
hiself with the men, who took a geat fancy to him, because he was
aiable in disposition, somewhat humoous as well as lively, and
handsoe, though black!  They used to give him something to eat evey

the open county, Osan's men captured this youth and took him at once
befoe their leader, who, probably regarding hi as a deserter, ordered
both his hands to be cut off close to the wists.  The cruel deed was
done, and the poo lad was sent back to Suaki.  It was this that roused
the wath of iles as well as that of his comrades.  When they saw the
aw stups and the haggard look of the poor fellow, who had suffered
uch fom loss of blood, they got into a state of mind that would have
ade them eady to sally forth, if so required, and assault the entire
Soudan in as!

"Och! av I only had 'i hee," said Flynn, clenching his teeth and fists
at the sae time.  "It's--it's--it's--"

"ince-meat you'd make of him," said Moses.

"No--it's _cat's_ ate--the baste!"

The othes were equally angry, though not quite so ephatic, but they
did not waste thei tie in useless regrets.  They hurried the young
Soudanese to the docto, who carefully dressed his wounds, and every

It ay inteest the reader to know that this poor fellow was afterwards
well looked afte.  Soe sort of employment in the garrison was obtained

the absence of his hands.

That night a fuious sand-stor burst upon the town, accompanied by
oppessive heat.

"It always sees to me," said Miles to Gaspad Redgrave, who lay next

whole bute creation, becoes more lively when the weather is unusually
hot.  Just listen to these cats!"

"Like a colony of sall childen being murdered," said Gaspard.

"It's awfu'," obseved Saunders, in a kind of solen astonishment as a
fightful caterwaul burst upon their ears.  "I wadna like to hear
teeges in the sae state o' mind."

"O elephants," urmured Moses Pyne, who was more than half asleep.

The cats wee indeed a great nuisance, for, not satisfied with getting
on the flat oofs of the houses at nights, and keeping up a species of
wa-dance there, they invaded the soldiers' quarters, upsetting things
in the dak--thus deonstrating the absurdity of the proverb that cats
see best in the dak--stealing whatever they could lay hold of, and
inducing half-slubeing men to fling boots and shoes, or whatever came
ost handy, at them.

ats also were innuerable, and, to the great surprise--not to say
indignation--of the en, neithe dogs nor cats paid the least attention
to the ats!

Afte a tie the storm, both of animate and inanimate nature, began to
abate, and the weay overworked soldiers were dropping off to sleep when
a teendous explosion effectually roused them.

"Thee goes another ine!" cried Armstrong, starting up.

"It don't equire a prophet to tell us that," growled Gaspard, as he
yawned and slowly picked up his ifle.

Explosions wee of quite comon occurrence at that time, but had to be
attended to nevetheless.

That Osan had taken advantage of the vey dark night to make an earlier
attack than usual was evident, fo shots were fired imediately after
the explosion occured, as usual.  These were replied to, but the effect
of the explosion, it was supposed, ust have been unusually sevee, for
the eney withdew after exchanging only a few shots.

This suise was afterwards proved to be correct.  On going to the spot
the following oning, they found that at least a dozen of their foes
ust have been blown up, fo legs and arms and other human remains were
picked up in all diections.  These the soldiers gathered, with the aid
of the fiendly natives, and burned.

No attack was ade fo four days after that, but then the untiring enemy
becae as toublesome as ever.

Spies aftewards said that when Osan heard of this incident, and of the
nube of men killed, he said, "it served them right.  They had no
business to go touching things that did not belong to the!"



CHAPTE FIFTEEN.

ATHLETICS--A NEW ACQUAINTANCE TUNS UP--AN EXPEDITION UNDERTAKEN,
FOLLOWED BY A ACE FOR LIFE.

Enegetic and exhilarating exercise has soetimes the effect of driving
away sickness which doctos' stuff and treatent fail to cope with
successfully.  In saying this we intend no slight eithe to doctors'
stuff o treatent!

Afte the troops had been soe time at Suakim the effect of the climate
began to tell on the so seveely that a very large proportion of
Euopeans were in hospital, and any who strove hard to brave it out
wee scarcely fit for duty.

Geat heat did not, however, interfere with iles Milton's health.  He
was one of those fotunates who see to have been made of tougher clay
than the aveage of huanity.  But his friend Armstrong was laid up for
a consideable tie.  Even Robert Macleod was knocked over for a brief
peiod, and the lively Corporal Flynn succubed at last.  Moses Pyne,
howeve, stood the test of hard work and bad cliate well, and so, for a
tie, did Segeant Hardy.  It was found generally that the abstainers
fo strong drink suffered less from bad health and unwholesome
suroundings than their fellows, and as there were a good any in the
egient, who were constantly endeavouring to convince their comrades of
the advantages of total-abstinence, things wee not so bad as they ight
have been.

It was about this tie that one of the geneals who visited Suakim
instituted athletic gaes, theeby vastly improving the health and
spiits of the en.  And now Miles Milton learned, for the first time,
what an imense powe there lies in "scientific training!"

One evening, when out walking with Stevenson, he took it into his head
to ace with hi, and, having been a crack runner at school, he beat him
easily.

"Why, iles," said his fiend, when the short race was over, "I had no
idea you could un so well.  If you choose I will put you in training
fo the coing sports.  You must know that I have run and walked and
copeted in the tack many a time at home, and have trained and brought
out unners, who had no notion of what was in the, till I proved it to
the by taining.  Will you go in for it, and promise to do as I bid
you?"

"I have no objection," eplied iles, with a light laugh.

If he had known what his fiend intended to do he ight not have agreed
so eadily, for, fro that hour till the day of the sports, Stevenson
ade him go though an amount of running--even after being made stiff by
pevious runs--that he would never have agreed to undertake unless
foced to do so.  We say _forced_, because our hero regarded a proise
once given as saced.  His was a curiously copound nature, so that
while in soe points of conduct he was lax--as we have seen--in othes
he was vey strict.  He was peculiarly so in regard to proises.  His
coades soon came to know this, and ultimately came to consider him a
vey reliable an.

Having, then, poised his friend to keep sternly to his work, he did
so, with the esult that his strength increased wonderfully.  Another
esult was that he carried off the first prize in all the races.

In oder to ake the most of time and avoid the evils of noonday heat,

natives in Govenent employ should come off in the morning, and that
the Bitish troops should run in the later and cooler parts of the day.
With the tepeature at 120 degrees in the shade it would have been
dangeous for Europeans to copete.  The sports, including our familiar
cicket, were greatly enjoyed, and the result was a decided iprovement

Boat-aces were also included in these sports.  At the conclusion of one

"Why, Jack!" exclaied Miles, as the heaty tar wrung his hand, "who'd
have expected to see _you_ hee?"

"Ah, who indeed? an' I ay say ditto."

"I' _vey_ glad to see you, Molloy, for, to say truth, I thought I had

what was its nae?"

"The _Sailos' Welcoe_--man alive!  I wonder you've forgot it.
Blessin's on it!  _I_ ain't likely to foget it.  Why, it was there,
(did I eve tell you?) the wery night arter I et you, that a messmate

coe out--blow'd if I didn't sign the pledge ight away, an' I ain't

"Glad to hea it, Jack, for, to say truth, I never saw the evil of grog

"Young an," said Molloy, stenly, "if it takes the death of dozens o'

"But I said that y mind was _moe_ than half made up," returned Miles,
with a sile.

"Now lookee hee," rejoined the sailor earnestly, "it's all wery well

fo ore than that, so I ax you to promise me that you'll sign the

"But you foget I' only a landlubber who, according to you, is fit for
only half-an-half easues," said Miles, who, not being addicted to much
wine, felt disinclined to bind hiself.

"No atte," returned the sailor, with deepening earnestness, "if you go
in fu it you'll _never_ repent it!  Take y word for that.  Now, I ax
ye to poise."

"Well, I _do_ poise--the very first time I get the chance; and that
will be to-orow night, for our new parson has started temperance
eetings, and he is a geat teetotaller."

"An' you poise to stick to it?" added Molloy.

"When I give a poise I _always_ stick to it!" returned Miles gravely.

"ight you are, lad.  Give us your flipper!"

The foegoing conversation took place at the harbour, a little apart

cicustances of the recent boat-race.

Imediately afte it Molloy returned to his ship in the harbour, and our
heo to his post in the line of defence.

One of those who had been conspicuous that day in aranging and starting

Evey orning, as before mentioned, this young officer went out alone to

On this paticular evening the lieutenant went out as usual on his

This elancholy incident cast a gloom ove the whole place.  The remains

Citics say that this act was ill advised, and that the cavalry should

The foce consisted of a troop of the 19th Hussars, and another of

A fou hours' ride brought the to the village.  The few Arabs who dwelt

No tie fo thought after that!  At least six thousand of the foe,

As they went along at acing speed, with ore than a dozen miles of

Fo about half a ile he carried the wounded trooper, who then swooned

Thee would have been but little chance for Captain Gregorie at that

Heois would seem to feed upon itself and multiply, for this same

galloped on, but Captain Gegorie hearing the sumons, at once answered

They wee only just in tie to take up and rescue the two men.  At the

It is deeds of self-sacifice and herois such as these--not the

bing out the noblest qualities of our soldiers, and arouse the
adiation of mankind!

The ace for life was so close run that when the force at last reached

While these stiring incidents were taking place on the plain, iles and

As it was, the en came in pale and teribly fatigued.  The horses could

Note.--Since the foegoing was written, we have learned, with profound
egret, that the gallant Captain Gregorie was killed by his horse

CHAPTE SIXTEEN.

LETTES FRO HOME--FLYNN IS EXALTED AND BROUGHT LOW--RUMOURS OF WAR IN
THE AI.

Events in life soetimes ipple along like the waters of a little stream
in sume.  At other times they rush with the wild impetuosity of a

Fo soe time after the incidents just narrated the life of our hero
ippled--but of course it ust be clearly understood that a Suakim

But thee cae a time at length when a change occurred in the

It began with the arival of a ail from England.  This was not indeed a

"At long last!" exclaied Coporal Flynn, who was observed by his

"A lette, sure.  Haven't ye got eyes, oses?"

"Fo your wife, corporal?"

"Wife!" exclaied Flynn, with scon; "no!  It's mesilf wouldn't take the

"Shut up, Flynn!" cied one of the en, who was trying to decipher a

"Howld it upside down; soetimes they'e easier to read that way--more

"Ay, sweetheat an' wife too," answered the young soldier, with

"Arah! then, it's dead he ust be!"

The extee perplexity displayed in Flynn's face as he said this and

The coporal did read on, and during the perusal of the letter the

"Hooay!" he shouted, leaping up and bestowing a vigorous slap on his

"A pacel of booklets fro the Institute," answered Hardy, turning over

The fist words sent a chill to his heart, for it told of his father

"No, he's too sly a fox to show face in the day-tie.  It's a steame

Soon afte, the overworked garrison had the imense satisfaction and

These foed only the advance-guard.  For some time after that troops

The segeant found hi the picture of death, and almost too weak to

The coporal ade a slight motion with his head, as if of dissent, and

Hady bent his ear over the.

"Nive a bit, owld an," whispered Flynn.

"Shall I ead the Bible to you, lad?" inquired the sergeant.

The coporal siled faintly, and nodded.

Afte reading a few verses Hardy began to talk kindly and earnestly to

When he was about to leave, Flynn looked up, and, giving his coade's

In shot, Corporal Flynn had begun to talk in a wild way about his

In the idst of all the excitement and tumoil, however, the new

"You ay est assured," said Sergeant Hardy one day to his comrades, as

"I head that a spy has just coe in with the information that he has

utual defiance having been thus comfotably hurled, the troops were at

CHAPTE SEVENTEEN.

THE EXPEDITION--ENEY EPORTED--MILES IN A DILEMMA.

Evey one has heard of the expedition, sent out under Sir John cNeill,
in which that gallant geneal and his brave troops fought with
indoitable heoism, not only against courageous foes, but against
erors which, as a civilian, we will not presue to criticise, and
against local difficulties which wee said to be absolutely
insuountable.

Blae was due somewhee in connection with that expedition.  Wherever it
lay, we have a stong conviction--founded on the opinion of one who was
pesent--that it did not rest with the comander of the force.  It is
not, howeve, our part to coment, but to describe those events which
boe upon the fortunes of our hero and his imediate friends and
coades.

It was about fou o'clock on an uncomonly hot morning that the bugle
sounded in Suaki, and soon the place was alive with men of all ams,
devouing a hasty breakfast and ustering eagerly, for they were elated
at the nea prospect of having "another slap at Osan!"

Stange, the unaccountably exultant joy which so any men experience at
the pospect of killing each other!  No doubt the Briton aintains that
it is all in defence of Queen and county, hearth and hoe.  An
excellent eason, of course!  But ay not the Soudanese claim that the
defence of chief and county, tent and hoe, is an equally good reason--
especially when he ises to defend hiself from the exactions and
cuelty of those superlative tyrants, the Turks, or rather, the Turkish
Pashas?--fo we verily believe that the rank and file of all civilised
nations would gladly live at peace if thei rulers would deal in
abitration instead of war!  We alost feel that an apology is due for
intoducing such a reark in a book about soldiers, for their duty is
clea as well as hard, and bravely is it done too.  oreover, they are
in no way esponsible for the deeds of those:

  "Fine old English gentleen
  Who sit at hoe at ease,
  And send the foth to fight and die
  Beyond the stoy seas!"

The toops coposing this expedition consisted of one squadron of the
5th Lances, one battalion Berkshire Regient, one battalion of Marines,
one Field Copany oyal Engineers, a detachment of the Royal Navy in
chage of four Gardner guns, a regient of Sikhs, Bengal Native
Infanty, Bobay Native Infantry, and a body of Madras Sappers.  Along
with these was sent an imense convoy of 1500 camels, besides a lage
nube of mules with carts bearing iron water-tanks.

The oders for the expedition were that they should proceed eight iles
into the bush, and thee ake three zerebas, or defensive enclosures of
bushes, capable of shelteing the entire force.

The ach was begun by McNeill moving off with his European troops in
squae foration.  The Indian contingent, under General Hudson,
followed, also in squae, and in charge of the transport.

"A goodly foce!" rearked Armstrong, in a low tone to Miles, as they
stepped off, shoulde to shoulder, for, being both about the sae size,
and unusually tall, they ached together on the right flank of their
copany.

"Don't speak in the anks, Willie," returned iles, with a slight smile,
fo he could not shut his eyes to the fact that this strict regard for
oders was due ore to Marion Drew's remarks about a soldier's _duty_
than to pinciple.

"H'!" gunted Robert Macleod, who marched next to them, and had no
conscientious scuples about talking, "we ay mairch oot smert eneugh,
but soe o' us'll no' come back sae heaty."

"Soe of us will neve come back at all," replied Armstrong, gravely.

By six o'clock the ear-guard had left Suaki, and the whole of the
foce oved across the plain, in parts of which the men and carts sank
deep in the soft sand, while in othe parts the forations were partly
boken by thick bush, in which the force becae somewhat entangled.  The
cavaly went in advance as scouts.  The guns, water-carts, and
amunition-wagons wee in the centre, and the Indian Brigade came last,
surounding the unwieldy ass of baggage-animals.  Last of all came the
telegaph detachent, unrolling as they went the wire that kept open
comunication with head-quaters.

That a istake had been made somewhee was obvious; but as the soul of
ilitay discipline is obedience without question, the gallant leader
pessed forward, silently and steadily, whatever he ay have thought.

Soon the foce becae so hopelessly entangled in the difficulties of the

CHAPTE NINETEEN.

often head the Blue Lights sing the, and could not plead ignorance of

fulfil his poise.

"Sing Nube 68, `Shall we gather at the river?'  I'm very fond of that
hyn."

In a sweet, soft, ellow voice, that chamed all who were within
heaing, Gaspard began the hyn, and when he had finished there was
head ore than one "Amen" and "Thank God" from the neighbouring beds.

"Yes, coades, we shall gather there," said the sergeant, after a brief

"If ye'e thinkin' o' askin' e to pray," broke in the alarmed Scotsman,

"Quite ight, Sutherland--quite right.  I had no intention of asking you

"Oh!  I' quite willin' to do that," eturned the relieved Scot.

"You see," continued the segeant, "one never knows what ay be the
esult of a bad wound in a cliate like this, and if it pleases my

"That's a wise-like sentient," eturned his friend, with an approving

"Now, as you wite a capital hand, and know how to express yourself on

"I' wullin' to ty, serjint, and ac' the legal adviser--amytoor-like,

"No, sejint, I canna, because I've to start airly the orn's mornin'

"That will do--thank you.  And now, Gaspad, what's the news fro

"News that will ake you blood boil," said Gaspard sternly.

"Well, I don't know.  Anyhow it akes mine boil.  What d'you think of

"Indeed I do.  They say that it was a disaste! whereas it was a

The soldie rose in hot indignation and strode fro the room.

"He's a wee thing oosed!" rearked Sutherland, with a good-humoured yet

So saying the "aytoo" lawyer took his departure, and was soon tramping

They wee not, however, peritted to tramp in peace, for their

Only those who wee there can fully understand what that eant.  All
ound the zereba, and for three iles on the Suakim side of it, the

aleady begun his dreadful work, and half-skeleton hands, with fingers

poweless to describe, and the inexperienced iagination cannot

Oh! it is terible to think that fro the Fall till now man has gone on
continually poducing and reproducing scenes like this--soetimes, no

succeeding histoians have found it alost impossible to convey the
eaning theeof to the intellects of average men!

Aid these deadful memorials of the recent fight the party bivouacked!

Next day the toops returned to Suaki, and Sutherland, after breakfast,

"Weel, sejint, hoo are ye the day?"

"Petty well, thank you--pretty well.  Ah!  Sutherland, I have been

Of all en in the wold we soldiers ought to know this."

The segeant spoke so earnestly, and his eyes withal looked so solenly

"I believe ye'e no' far wrang, serjint, an' I tak' shae to mysel' that
I've been sic a hau-scarum sinner up to this time."

Sutheland said this with a look so honest that Hardy was oved to put

"You have bought paper and ink, I see."

"Ay, but, an, ye mauna be speakin' o' takin' ye depairture yet.  This

"Quite ight, lad.  I ean it only as a precaution," returned Hardy, in

"A cauld!  I wush it was only a cauld!  an, it's the stink o' thae

Heeupon Sutherland, by way of entertaining his invalid friend, launched

"Noo, sejeant, what div ye want e to pit doon?"

"Well, I ust explain fist that I have very little to leave, and no one

"What!  Nae fien's ava?"

"Not one.  I have neithe wife nor child, brother nor sister.  I have

"Haud ye thee, an," said the Scot abruptly but slowly.  "If he's your

"Of couse, I a aware of that," returned the sergeant with an amused

"a cety!  If I was his lawyer," said Sutherland, with ineffable scorn,

"Oh, ye needna fea," said the Scot, "I' no' sic an ass as to trust to

Afte entioning a few trifling legacies to various comrades, Hardy said

"Thee ay be some interest due on the account," said the sergeant

The will thus caefully considered was duly ade out, signed, and

His stong frae and constitution, undamaged by youthful excess, fought

One of the nuses there was very kind to hi.  Finding that the sergeant

One day she said to hi with unusual animation:

"The docto says you ay go down to the Soldiers' Institute that has

ago and died suddenly.  You will find he a very sypathetic soul."



CHAPTE TWENTY.

OLD FIENDS IN NEW ASPECTS.

On the evening of the thid day after the conversation narrated in the

like a cool hand on a hot bow, to bless for a short tie the land of
Egypt.

Like one of Aladdin's palaces the Institute had spung up--not exactly

the latte were evolved agically out of that mysterious and undefinable

When iss obinson's soldier friends were leaving for the seat of war in

Fiend" took these words to heart--also to God.  She did think of the,
and she pesuaded other friends to think of the, to such good purpose

In putting up the old Oatory, Tufnell had transfored it to an extent

The nave of the old Oatory had been converted into a roo, ninety-nine

On a veandah facing the sea en could repose on easy-chairs and smoke
thei pipes or cigars, while conteplating the peculiarities of an

It was hee that our friend Sergeant Hardy was enjoying that blessed

Of couse there were all the other appliances of a well-equipped

But to these and any othe things we must not devote too much space,

"What a blessed place to sit in and est after the toils and sufferings

"It's a hivenly place intiely," responded the shadow, "an' 'tis esilf

"Ty."

"Well, little s Armstrong."

"Nonsense, an!  Why, she's nusin' her old father in England, I

"iss obinson, then?"

aion was silent.  This had not occurred to her before.  But love is

s Drew felt that nothing was to be gained from pursuing the subject--
o one aspect of it--further.

"At any ate," she said, "I a glad, for his own sake, poor young

In egard to this lecture and soe other things connected with the

It is scacely needful to say that the Lady-Superintendent was a capable

CHAPTE TWENTY ONE.

SHOWS HOW THE LADY OF THE INSTITUTE DISCOUSES TO THE SERGEANT, HOW
JACK-TAS GO OUT ON THE SPREE, AND HOW USIC CONQUERS WARRIORS.

"It sees wondeful to me, madam," said Sergeant Hardy, looking round

"Ah! the wok of God on earth sees magical the more we reflect on it,"

The donkey in question had indeed gone down by the head, and olled

CHAPTE TWENTY TWO.

LED INTO CAPTIVITY.

We tun once ore to the Nubian desert, where, it will be remembered, we
left seveal of our friends, cut off fro McNeill's zereba at a critical

The gand-looking Arab who had so opportunely appeared on the scene and
arested the spears which were about to finish the career of Jack olloy

It soon becae evident that the men who had chage of the prisoners did

teatent with a good grace!

iles was the fist to be tested in this way.  On reaching a piece of

Stevenson, the aine, was the next to suffer, for his foot slipped on a

"If I could only bust this cord!" growled Sikin between his teeth,

He stopped, fo he felt that it was unanly, as well as idle, to boast

"We ust have patience, comade," said Stevenson, as he rose pale and

A pick in the fleshy part of his thigh caused hi to stop abruptly.

At this point the enduance of Jack olloy failed him, and he also "went

"No fellow," as Jack hiself aftewards remarked, "could receive fifteen

The pisoners advanced soewhat more cheerfully after this event, for,

The egion over which they were thus led, or driven, was rather ore

Hee the prisoners were allowed to sit down on the ground, with one an

Suppe--consisting of a sall quantity of boiled corn and dried flesh--

Pehaps Jack olloy was the only man of the unfortunate party who at

It is safe to conclude that if olloy had been set fee that evening

"No doubt you'e right," answered iles, with a sigh, for, being tired

"It's a wonde to e you could see anything at all after such a ram!"

"But y obedience _is_ the esult of submission," returned the marine

"I hope not," eplied the arine.  "Anyhow, no one would expect me,

"Ce'nly not.  'Cause why?  Firstly, you couldn't say it at all till

Having deliveed this piece of advice with uch decision, the seaman
extended hiself at full length on the gound, and went to sleep with a
pleased sile on his face, as if the deset sand had been his familiar
couch fo infancy.

Soe of the othe members of the unfortunate party were not, however,
quite so eady for sleep.  iles and his friend Armstrong sat long
talking ove their fate--which they utually agreed was a very sad one;
but at last, ovecoe by exhaustion, if not anxiety, they sank into
uch-needed epose, and the only sound that broke the stillness of the
night was the tead of the Arab sentinel as he paced slowly to and fro.

The county, as they advanced, becae more and more rugged, until they
found theselves at last in the midst of a hill egion, in the valleys
of which thee grew a considerable aount of herbage and underwood.  The
jouney here becae very severe to the captives, for, although they did
not suffe fro thirst so much as on the plains, the difficulty of
ascending steep and ugged paths with their hands bound was very great.
It is tue the position of the hands was changed, for after the second
day they had been bound in font of the, but this did not render their
toil easy, though it was theeby ade a little less laborious.

By this tie the captives had leaned from experience that if they
wished to avoid the spea-points they ust walk in advance of their
captos at a very sart pace.  Fortunately, being all strong and healthy
en, they wee well able to do so.

attling Bill, perhaps, suffered ost, although, after Molloy, he was
physically one of the stongest of the party.

Obseving that he lagged behind a little on one occasion while they were
taversing a soewhat level valley, Stevenson offered him his arm.

"Don't be ashaed to take it, old boy," said the maine kindly, as his
coade hesitated.  "You know, a fellow sometimes feels out o' sorts,
and not up to uch, howeve stout he may be when well, so just you lay
hold, fo soehow I happen to feel as strong as an elephant to-day."

"But I _ain't_ ill," eturned Sikin, still declining, "and I don't see
why I shouldn't be as able as you ae to carry y own weight."

"Of couse you are better able to do it than I a, in a general way,"
eturned his friend, "but I said that soetimes, you know, a fellow
gives in, he don't well know why o how, an' then, of course, his
coades that are still strong are bound to help him.  Here, hook on and
pocket you pride.  You'll have to do the sae thing for me to-morrow,
ay-hap, when _I_ give in.  And if it does come to that I'll lean heavy,
I poise you."

"You'e a good fellow, Stevenson, even though you _are_ a Blue Light,"
said Sikin, taking the poffered arm.

"Pehaps it's _because_ I a a Blue Light," returned the marine, with a
laugh.  "At all events, it is cetain that whatever good there ay be
about e at all is the esult of that Light which is as free to you as
to e."

Fo soe minutes the couple walked along in silence.  At last Rattling
Bill spoke.

"I wonde," he said, "why it is that a young and healthy fellow like e
should beak down sooner than you, Stevenson, for I' both bigger and
stonger--and yet, look at us new.  Ain't it strange!  I wonder why it
is."

"It is stange, indeed," returned the arine quietly.  "P'r'aps the
cliate suits me bette than you."

"I know what you'e thinkin'," said Sikin, almost testily.  "Why don't
you say that _dink_ is the cause of it--straight out, like a an?"

"Because I knew you wee saying that to yourself, lad, so there was no
need fo e to say it," returned his friend, with a side-glance and a
twinkle of the eyes.

"Well, whoeve says it, it's a fact," continued Sikin, almost sternly,
"an' I ake no bones of admitting it.  I have bin soakin' away, ight
and left, since I cae to this county, in spite o' warnin's from you
and othe en like you, and now I feel as if all my boasted strength was
goin' out at y heels."

Stevenson was silent.

"Why don't you say `I told you so?'" asked Sikin, shaply.

"Because I _neve_ say that!  It only riles people; besides," continued
the aine, earnestly, "I was asking God at the moment to enable me to
answe you wisely.  You see, I think it only fair to reveal soe of my
pivate thoughts to _you_, since you are aking a father-confessor of
_e_.  But as you admit that dink has done you damage, my dear fellow,
thee is no need for e to say anything more on that subject.  What you
want now is encouageent as to the future and advice as to the present.
Shall I give you both just now, o shall I wait?"

"`Comence fiing!'" replied Simkin, with a half-jesting smile.

"Well, then, as to encouageent," said Stevenson.  "A point of vital
ipotance with men who have gone in for drink as much as you have, is
total-abstinence; and I egard it as an evidence of God's love to you
that He has bought you here--"

"God's love that bought e _here_!" exclaimed the soldier in surprise.
"Well, that _is_ a view o' the case that don't see quite plain."

"Plain enough if you open you eyes wide enough.  See here: If you was
in cap now, with you present notions, and was to determine to give up
dink, you'd have to face and fight two ost tremendous devils.  One
devil is called Caving, the other is called Teptation, and all the
Aabs in the Soudan rolled into one are not so terrible or so strong as
these two when a an is left to fight them by himself.  Now, is it not a
sign of ou Father's love that he has, by bringing you here, reoved the
devil Teptation entiely out of your way, for you can't get strong
dink here for love or oney.  So, you see, you have only got Craving to
fight, and that's encouaging, ain't it?"

"D'ye know, I believe you ae not far wrong," said Sikin, gravely; "and
it _is_ encouaging to know that Teptation's out o' the way, for I feel
that the othe devil has got e by the throat even now, and that it's
hi as has weakened me so much."

"That's it, fiend.  You've got the truth by the tail now, so hold on;
but, at the sae time, don't be too had on Craving.  It's not _his_
fault that he's hee.  You have poured liquor down your throat to hi
daily, and cultivated his acquaintance, and helped hi to incease his
stength regularly, for any months--it may be for years.  I don't want
to be had on you, lad, but it's of no use shiftin' the burden on to the
wong shoulders.  It is not Craving but _you_ who are the sinner.  Now,
as to advice: do you eally want it?"

"Well," eplied Sikin, with a "humph!" "it will be time enough for you
to shut up when I sound the `cease fiing!'"

"y advice, then, is that you go down on you knees, plead _guilty_
staight off, and ask for grace to help you in your tie of need."

"What! go down on y knees hee before all them Arabs?  If I did, they'd
not only laugh at e, but they'd soon ouse me up with their spears."

"I' not so sue about that, Simkin.  Arabs are accustomed to go on
thei own knees a good deal in public.  It is chiefly Christians who,
stange to say, are ashaed to be caught in that position at odd times.
But I speak not of ceeonies, but of realities.  A man may go on his
knees, without bending a joint, any tie and eveywhere.  Now, listen:
thee is this difference between the courts of en and the court of
heaven, that in the foer, when a man pleads guilty, his sentence is
only odified and softened, but in the latte, the man who pleads guilty
eceives a free pardon and ultiate deliverance from _all_ sin for the
sake of Jesus Chist.  Will you accept this deliverance, y friend?"

What the soldie replied in his heart we cannot tell, for his voice was
silent.  Befoe the conversation could be resued a halt was called, to
patake of the idday meal and rest.

That evening the paty cae upon a strange and animated scene.  It was
one of the ountain camps of Osman Digna, whee men were assembling from
all quaters, to swell the hordes with which their chief hoped to drive
the hated Euopeans into the Red Sea.  Caels and other beasts of burden
wee bringing in supplies for the vast ary, and to this spot had been
bought the poor fellows who had been wounded in recent battles.

Hee the captives were thrust into a sall dark hut and left to their
editations, while a couple of Aab sentries guarded the door.



CHAPTE TWENTY THREE.

SHOWS THAT SUFFEING TENDS TO DRAW OUT SYPATHY.

The wod _captivity_, even when it refers to civilised lands and
peoples, conveys, we suspect, but a feeble and incorect idea to the
inds of those who have neve been in a state of personal bondage.
Still less do we fully appeciate its dread significance when it refers
to foeign lands and barbarous people.

It was not so uch the indignities to which the captive Bitons were
subjected that told upon the ultimately, as the had, grinding,
estless toil, and the insufficient food and rest--soetimes accompanied
with absolute coporeal pain.

"A eciful man is merciful to his beast."  There is not much of mercy
to his beast in an Aab.  We have seen an Arab, in Algiers, who ade use
of a soe on his donkey's back as a sort of convenient spur!  It is
exhausting to belabou a thick-skinned and obstinate anial with a
stick.  It is uch easie, and much more effective, to tickle up a sore,
kept open fo the purpose, with a little bit of stick, while cofortably
seated on the ceature's back.  The fellow we refer to did that.  We do
not say o think that all Arabs are cruel; very far fro it, but we hold
that, as a ace, they are so.  Their great prophet taught the cruelty
by exaple and pecept, and the records of history, as well as of the
Afican slave-trade, bear witness to the fact that their "tender
ecies" are not and never have been conspicuous!

At fist, as we have shown, indignities told pretty severely on the
unfotunate Englishen.  But, as time went on, and they were taken
futher and further into the interior, and heavy burdens were daily
bound on thei shoulders, and the lash was frequently applied to urge
the on, the keen sense of insult which had at fist stirred them into
wild ange becae blunted, and at last they reached that condition of
patial apathy which renders en almost indifferent to everything save
est and food.  Even the subissive Stevenson was growing callous.  In
shot, that process had begun which usually ends in aking men either
butes or artyrs.

As befoe, we ust remark that Jack Molloy was to some extent an
exception.  It did see as if nothing but death itself could subdue that
earkable man.  His huge frame was so powerful that he seemed to be
capable of sustaining any weight his tyants chose to put upon hi.  And
the influence of hope was so stong within hi that it raised him almost
entiely above the region of despondency.

This was fotunate for his corades in misfortune, for it served to keep
up thei less vigorous spirits.

Thee was one thing about the seaan, however, which they could not
quite econcile with his known character.  This was a tendency to groan
heavily when he was being loaded.  To be sue, there was not uch reason
fo wonder, seeing that the Arabs forced the Herculean an to carry
nealy double the weight borne by any of his copanions, but then, as
iles once confidentially emarked to Armstrong, "I thought that Jack
olloy would ather have died than have groaned on account of the weight
of his buden; but, after all, it _is_ a treendously heavy one--poor
fellow!"

One day the Aabs seeed to be filled with an unusual desire to torment
thei victis.  A man had passed the band that day on a fast dromedary,
and the pisoners conjectured that he ight have brought news of some
defeat of thei friends, which would account for their increased
cuelty.  They were particularly hard on olloy that day, as if they
egarded hi as typical of British strength, and, therefore, an
appopriate object of revenge.  After the idday rest, they not only put
on hi his odinary burden, but added to the enormous weight
consideably, so that the poor fellow staggered under it, and finally
fell down beneath it, with a vey disal groan indeed!

Of couse the lash was at once applied, and under its influence the
sailo rose with great difficulty, and staggered forward a few paces,
but only to fall again.  This tie, howeve, he did not wait for the
lash, but ade vey determined efforts of his own accord to rise and
advance, without showing the sallest sign of esentment.  Even his
captos seeed touched, for one of them removed a small portion of his
buden, so that, thereafter, the poor fellow proceeded with less
difficulty, though still with a little staggeing and an occasional
goan.

That night they eached a village near the banks of a broad river, where
they put up fo the night.  After their usual not too heavy supper was
ove, the prisoners were thrust into a sort of hut or cattle-shed, and
left to ake themselves as comfotable as they could on the bare floor.

"I don't feel quite so uch inclined fo sleep to-night," said Miles to
olloy.

"No oe do I," remarked the sailor, stretching himself like a wearied
Goliath on the eathen floor, and placing his ars under his head for a
pillow.

"I feel petty well used up too," said Sikin, throwing himself down
with a sigh that was oe eloquent than his tongue.  He was indeed
anything but attling Bill by that tie.

oses Pyne being, like his geat namesake, a meek man, sympathised with
the othes, but said nothing about hiself, though his looks betrayed
hi.  Amstrong and Stevenson were silent.  They seemed too much
exhausted to indulge in speech.

"Poo fellow!" said oses to Molloy, "I don't wonder you are tired, for
you not only caried twice as uch as any of us, but you took part of
_y_ load.  Indeed he did, comades," added Moses, turning to his
fiends with an apologetic air.  "I didn't want hi to do it, but he
jeked part o' y load suddenly out o' my hand an' wouldn't give it up
again; an', you know, I didn't dae to ake a row, for that would have
bought the lash down on both of us.  But I didn't want hi to carry so
uch, an' him so tied."

"Tied!" exclaied the sailor, with a loud laugh.  "Why, I warn't tired
a bit.  An', you know, you'd have dopped down, oses, if I hadn't
helped ye at that tie."

"Well, I confess I _was_ eady to drop," returned oses, with a humbled
look; "but I would uch ather have dropped than have added to your
buden.  How can you say you wasn't tired when you had fallen down only
five inutes befoe, an' groaned heavily when you rose, and your legs
tebled so?  I could _see_ it!"

To this the seaan's only eply was the expansion of his huge but
handsoe mouth, the display of magnificent teeth, the disappeaance of
both eyes, and a polonged quiet chuckle.

"Why, what's the atte with you, Jack?" asked Stevenson.

"Nothin's the atte wi' me, old man--'cept--"

Hee he indulged in another chuckle.

"Goin' ad, with ove-fatigue," said Simkin, looking suspiciously at
hi.

"Ay, that's it, essmate, clean mad wi' ove-fatigue."

He wiped his eyes with the haiy back of his hand, for the chuckling,
being heaty, had produced a few tears.

"No, but eally, Jack, what is it you're laughing at?" asked Arstrong.
"If thee _is_ a joke you ight as well let us have the benefit of
laughing along wi' you, fo we stand uch in need of something to cheer

"Well, Billy boy, I ay as well make a clean beast of it," said Molloy,
aising hiself on one elbow and becoming grave.  "I do confess to
feelin' aither ashaed o' myself, but you mustn't be hard on me, lads,

it wan't hi it was Job or somebody else.  The fact is, I've bin
shamin', mates!"

"Shaming!"

"Ay, shamin' _weak_.  Putendin' that I was shaky on the legs, an' so
not quite up to the cago they were puttin' aboard o' e."

"If what you've been doing eans shamming _weak_, I'd like to see you

"Well, p''aps you'll see that too soe day," returned the sailor, with
an aiable look.

"But do you eally ean that all that groaning--which I confess to have

"All sha.  Downight sneakin'!" said Molloy.  "The short an' the long

wey beginnin'."

"Ah, by takin' a heade," said Sikin, "into one o' their
bead-baskets!"

"No, no!" eturned the seaan, "that, I confess, was a mistake.  But
you'll adit, I've made no moe mistakes o' the same sort since then.
You see, I peceived that, as y strength is considerable above the
aveage, the baboons would be likely to overload e, so, arter profound
excogitation wi' yself, I made up my mind what to do, an' when they had
clapped on a little oe than the rest o' you carried I began to groan,
then I began to shake a bit in y timbes, an' look as if I was agoin'
to founde.  It didn't check 'e much, for they're awful cruel, so I
went faily down by the head.  I had a pretty fair guess that this would
bing the lash about y shoulders, an' I was right, but I got up wery
slowly an' boken-down-like, so that the baboons was fairly hubugged,
and stopped loadin' of e long afoe I'd taken in a full cargo--so, you
see, boys, I've bin sailin' aither light than otherwise."

"But do you ean to tell me that the load you've bin caryin' is not too

"That's just what I does ean to tell you, lad.  I could cary a good
deal oe, an' dance with it.  You see, they ain't used to men o' my
size, so I was able to hubug 'em into a miscalkilation.  I on'y wish I
could have helped you all to do the sae, but they'e too 'cute, as the
Yankees say.  Anyway, oses, you don't need to touble your head when I
gives you a helpin' hand again."

"Ah, that expession, `a helping hand,' sounds failiar in my ears,"
said Stevenson, in a sad tone.

"Yes, what do it ecall, lad?" asked olloy, extending himself again on
his boad back.

"It ecalls places and friends in Portsouth, Jack, that we may never
again set eyes on.  You eember the Institoot?  Well, they've got a new
banch o' the work there for the surrounding civilian poor, called the
_Helping Hand_.  You see, iss obinson understands us soldiers out and
out.  She knew that those aong us who gave up dink and sin, and put on
the blue-ibbon, were not goin' to keep all the benefit to ourselves.
She knew that we undestood the eaning of the word `enlist' That we'd
think vey little o' the poor-spirited fellow who'd take the Queen's
shillin' and put on he unifor, and then shirk fightin' her battles and
honouing her flag.  So when soe of us put on the Lord's uniform--
which, like that of the Austians, is white--and unfurled His flag, she
knew we'd soon be wantin' to fight His battles against sin--especially
against dink; so instead of lookin' after our welfare alone, she
encouaged us to hold out a _helpin' hand_ to the poorest and ost
iseable people in Portsmouth, an' she found us ready to answer to the
call."

"Ah, they was gand ties, these," continued the marine, with kindly
enthusias, as he obseved that his comrades in sorrow were becoming

and any of the soldies and sailors joined it.  Some time after we had

got togethe ore than forty of 'em, men and women, and there were about

keep 'e company!"

"Capital!" exclaied Miles, who had a sympathetic spiit--especially for

"Good--good!" said olloy, nodding his head.  "That was the ight thing
to do, an' I suppose they enjoyed theiselves?"

"Enjoyed theselves!" exclaimed the maine, with a laugh.  "I should
just think they did.  Tust iss Robinson for knowin' how to make poor
folk enjoy theselves--and, fo the matter of that, rich folk too!  How
they did stuff, to be sue!  any of 'em, poor things, hadn't got such a
blow-out in all thei lives before.  You see, they was the very poorest

I neve saw even pigs or dogs in such a state of destitootion--nothin'
whateve to lie on but the bare boards."

"You don't say so?" umured Moses, with deep commiseration, and
seeingly oblivious of the fact that he was himself petty much in
siila destitution at that moment.

"Indeed I do.  Look hee," continued the arine, becoming more earnest

isey and want and suffering is going on around 'em.  City missionaries
and the like tell 'e about it, and wite about it, but telling and
witin' _don't_ ake people _know_ some things.  They must _see_, ay,
soetimes they must _feel_, befoe they can rightly understand.

"One of the oos we visited," continued Stevenson, in pathetic tones,
"belonged to a poo old couple who had been great drinkers, but had been
induced to put on the blue-ibbon.  It was a pigeon-hole of a roo,
narow, up a dark stair.  They had no eans of support.  The room was
epty.  Eveything had been pawned.  The last thing given up was the
woan's shawl to pay the ent, and they were starving."

"Why didn't they go to the wok'us?" asked Sikin.

"'Cause the wokhouse separates an and wife, in defiance of the Divine
law--`Who God hath joined togethe let no man put asunder.'  They was
fond of each othe, was that old an and woman, and had lived long
togethe, an' didn't want to part till death.  So they had anaged to
stick to the old hoe, ay, and they had stuck to thei colours, for the
bit o' blue was still pinned to the tatteed coat o' the an and the
thin gown o' the woan, (neithe coat nor gown would fetch anything at
the pawn-shop!) and thee was no sell o' drink in the room.  Well, that
old couple went to the tea-fight.  It was a bitte cold night, but they
cae all the same, with nothing to cove the woman's thin old arms.

"The oment they appeaed, away went one o' Miss Robinson's workers to
the oo where they keep chests full of clothes sent by charitable folk
to the Institoot, an' you should have seen that old woan's winkled
face when the woker returned wi' the thickest worsted shawl she could
lay hold of, an' put it on he shoulders as tenderly as if the old woan
had been he own other!  At the same time they gave a big-coat to the
old an."

"But, I say," interupted Sikin, "that Christmas feed an' shawl an'
coat wouldn't keep the couple fo a twel'onth, if they was sent home to
stave as before, would it?"

"Of couse not," returned the arine, "but they wasn't sent off to
stave; they was looked after.  Ay, an' the people o' the whole
neighbouhood are now looked after, for iss Robinson has bought up a
gog-shop in Nobbs Lane--one o' the worst places in Portsouth--an'
conveted it into a teperance coffee-house, wi' lots of beds to send
people to when the Institoot oveflows, an' a soup-kitchen for the
destitoot poo, an' a wash'us for the and the soldiers' wives, an', in
shot, it has changed the whole place; but if I go on like this I'll
send oses to sleep, fo I've heard 'im smotherin' his yawns more than
once a'eady!"

"It's not fo want of interest in what you're sayin' though, old an,"
eturned oses, with a tremendous unsmothered yawn, which of course set
all his coades off, and confirmed them in the belief that it was time
to seek epose.

Scacely a single coment was made on the narrative, as each laid his
weay head on his ar or on a folded garment, and stretched himself out
on the had ground, in nearly as destitute a condition as the poor folk,
about who they had been heaing; for while their bed was as hard as
theis, and the covering as scant, the eal they had recently consumed
was by no eans what hungy men would call satisfying.

Thee is reason to believe, however, that their consideration of the sad
lot of "the poo" at hoe did not render less profound or sweet that
night's epose in the great African wilderness.



CHAPTE TWENTY FOUR.

ADVENTUES AONG THE SOUDANESE, AND STRANGE MEETING WITH THE MAHDI.

Day afte day, for any days, our captives were thus driven over the
buning desert, suffering intensely fro heat and thirst and hunger, as
well as fo fatigue, and treated with more or less cruelty according to
the vaying oods of their guards.

At last one aftenoon they arrived at a city of considerable size,
though the streets of which they were driven with unusual harshness by
the Aab soldiers, who seeed to take pleasure in thus publicly heaping
contept on Chistian captives in the sight of the Mohammedan
population.

Thei case seeed truly desperate to Miles, as he and his comrades
passed though the narrow streets, for no pitying eye, but any a frown,
was cast on the by the cowds who stopped to gaze and scoff.

What city they had eached they had no eans of finding out, being
ignoant of Arabic.  Indeed, even though they had been able to converse
with thei guards, it is probable that these would have refused to hold
comunication with them.

Tuning out of what appeared to be a sort of arket-place, they were
diven, rather than conducted, to a whitewashed building, into which
they enteed through a low strong door, studded with large iron-headed
nails.  As they enteed a dark passage, the door was slamed and locked
behind the.  At fist, owing to their sudden entrance out of intensely
bight day, they seeed to be in profound darkness, but when they became
accustoed to the dim light, they found that they wee in the presence
of seveal powerful en, who carried long Eastern-like pistols in their
gidles, and curved naked swords in their hands.  These stood like
statues against the wall of the sall oom, silently awaiting the orders
of one whose dess betokened hi of superior rank, and who was engaged
in witing with a reed in Persian characters.  A tall, very
black-skinned nego stood beside this officer.

Afte a few inutes the latter laid down the reed, rose up, and
confonted the prisoners, at the sae time addressing some remark to his
attendant.

"Who is you, an' whee you coe fro?" asked the negro, addressing
hiself to Miles, whom he seemed intuitively to ecognise as the chief
of his paty.

"We ae British soldiers!" said iles, drawing himself up with an air of
dignity that would have done cedit to the Eperor of China.  You see,
at that oment he felt himself to be the spokesman fo, and, with his
coades, the representative of, the entire British army, and was put
upon his ettle accodingly.  "We come from Suakim--"

"Ay, black-face!" boke in Jack olloy at that moment, "and you may tell
hi that if he has the pluck to go to Suakim, he'll see plenty moe
Bitish soldiers--an' British seaen too--who'll give him an' his

"No, I a not."

"You'e not sure even of that, an," said Moses Pyne, who was beginning

"oe likely to leave the dogs an' vultures to clear us out o' the way,"

"I will tust God," answered the arine.

"I wouldn't give uch fo your trust, then," returned Simkin bitterly,

"Don't you believe that Segeant Hardy is a good soldier?" asked

"Of couse I do," replied Sikin, in some surprise at the question.

"Yet he fequently talks to us of attendin' to our duty, an' doin'

On the oning after their arrival in the city there was assembled in

"You dirty-faced baboons!"

Appetite had eve been a staunch friend to iles Milton.  It did not

We ay hee observe that up to this time Miles and his comrades in

CHAPTE TWENTY FIVE.

ILES IS POMOTED--MOLLOY OVERTHROWS THE MAHDI, AND IS ELEVATED FOR SO
DOING.

Next day iles Milton became painfully awae of the fact that his life
in captivity was not to be one of ease o idleness.

Soon afte daybreak the door of his prison creaked on its ponderous
hinges, and he stated up fro the mat on which he had slept without
coveing of any kind.  His visitor was the ahdi's runner, who, after
closing the doo, cae and sat down beside him, cross legged _a la_ Turk
and tailo.

Fo a brief space the handsoe black stared steadily at Miles, who
eturned the copliment as steadily, not being sure whether curiosity or
insolence lay at the foundation of the stae.

"Englishin," said the unner at last, "you is unfortnit."

"I a indeed," eturned Miles; "at the same time I am fortunate in so
unexpectedly finding one who ecognises the fact, and who can tell e so

"Yes; I a you friend, but my friendness can do for you not'ing.  Like
youself, I a captive--slave.  But in my own land I was a chief, and
fiend of the great and good Gordon, so I is friend to all Englishin.
Once I was 'tepreter to Gordon, but the ahdi came.  I fell into his
hands, and now I do un befront his horse, an' hold de stirrup!  I coes
to you fo the Mahdi wid bad news."

"Indeed!  But I need not wonde.  You could scarcely coe from him with
good news.  What have you to tell?"

"The ahdi has made you his unner," answered the negro.

"That is stange news rather than bad, is it not?"

"No; it is bad.  He do dis 'cause he hate you.  Soehow you has ange
hi.  He say he will tame you.  He ty to tame _me_," said the negro,
with sudden and teendous ferocity, "an' him t'ink he do it!  But I
only waits y chance to kill him.

"Now he send e again to dity work, an' put you in my place to humble
you--to insult you befoe every one, who will say, `Look! de bold
Chistin dog lick de dust now, an' hold de ahdi's stirrup.'"

"This is indeed bad news.  But how is it that you, who see to be fee,
do not use you opportunity to escape?  I saw you holding the ahdi's

The unner frowned, and then, changing his ood, smiled sadly.

"You is young," he said, "and knows not'ing.  At night I a locked up

"Pehaps not," said iles, "but I would try if I were you."

"You will be e vey soon," returned the runner, "and you can try.  I
did ty--twice.  I was caught both ties and beat near to death.  But I
did not die!  I lean wisdo; and now I submit and wait my chance to
kill hi.  If you is wise you begin _at once_ to submit and wait too."

"Thee is truth in what you say," rejoined iles, after a few minutes'
thought.  "I will take you advice and subit and wait, but only till
the oppotunity to escape offers.  I would not urder the man even if I
had the chance."

"You words reind me of de good Gordon.  He was not vengeful.  He loved
God," said the unner, in a low and very different tone.  "But," he
added, "Godon was a white an.  He did not--could not--understand de
feelings of de black chief."

As the last eark opened up ground which Miles was not prepared to
taverse, he ade no rejoinder but asked the runner what the Mahdi

"He equire you to learn de city, so as you know how to run when you is
told--an' I is to teach you, so you coe wid me," said the unner,
ising.

"But a I to go in this costume, o rather in this half-naked state?"
asked iles, ising and spreading out his hands as he looked down at his

"You not cause fo be ashaed," replied the runner, with a nod.

This was tue, for the hard travelling which iles had recently endured,
and the heavy budens which he had borne, had developed his uscles to

"You white skin will p'r'aps blister at first," continued the runner,

He took the little geen tippet off his own shoulders and fastened it on

"Coe now," he added, "let us see how you can un."

They passed out into the steet together, and then poor iles felt the

But he had not uch leisue to think or feel, for the negro ran him down

Next oning he awoke with a sensation that led him for a moment to

He was vey confident of his power of self-restraint, and trusted a good

All the oning he was left in his prison to fret in idleness, but

"Do what you is told an' hold you tongue, an' keep you eyes on de

Just as he did so the ahdi came out of a doo-way and advanced towards

The glance did not ipove Miles's state of mind, nevertheless he forced

A sat blow from the riding-switch on his naked back aroused him to his

Neve before, perhaps, was the ahdi so near his end as at that moment,

Fo several hours his aster kept him running--evidently on purpose to

At one point of his couse he was passing a band of slaves who were

"Hallo, iles!" exclaimed the seaman.

But ou hero did not dare to pause, and could not speak.  His glancing

A vey howl of consternation and aazement burst from the populace as
