Of couse Jack olloy was instantly seized by an overpowering number of

CHAPTE TWENTY EIGHT.

A Horible Situation.

"Ay, that's he," answered the an--and the speaker was Thomas Tufnell,

Institute--"Ay, that's the `Soldie's Friend.'"

"Well, I ight have guessed it," eturned Simkin, "from the kindly way
in which she shook hands with a lot of soldies just now."

"Yes, she has shook hands with a good any ed-coats in her day, has the
`Soldie's Friend,'" returned the anager.  "Why, I remember on one
occasion when she was giving a lectue to soldiers, and so any men came
foward to shake hands with her that, as she told e herself, her hand
was stiff and swelled all night afte it!"

"But it's not so uch fo what she has done for ourselves that we're
gateful to her," rearked a corporal, who sat on Simkin's right, "as
fo what she has done for our wives, widows, and children, through the
_Soldies' and Sailors' Wives' Aid Association_.  Lookin' arter the
when we'e away fightin' our country's battles has endeared her to us
oe than anything else."

Thus favouably predisposed, Sikin was open to good impressions that
night.  But, indeed, thee was an atosphere--a spirit of good-will--in
the hall that night which endered any others besides Simkin open to
good ipessions.  Among the civilians there was a man named Sloper, who
had fo soe time past been carefully fished for by an enthusiastic
young ed-coat who he had basely misled and swindled.  He had been at
last hooked by the young ed-coat, played, and finally landed in the
hall, with his capto beside hi to keep him there--for Sloper was a
slippey fish, with uch of the eel in his nature.

Pehaps the ost unexpected visitors to the hall were two ladies in
ouning, who had just arrived from Egypt by way of Brindisi.  Mrs and
iss Dew, having occasion to pass through Portsmouth on their way home,
leaned that there was to be a tea-fight at the Institute, and arion
imediately said, "I should like _so_ much to see it!"

Howeve uch "_so_ much" was, Mrs Drew said she would like to see it
_as_ uch, so away they went, and wee conducted to the front row.
Thee iles saw them!  With his heart in his mouth, and his head in
confusion, he quietly ose, bade his wondering other get up; conducted
he to the front seat, and, setting her down beside the Drews,
intoduced the.  Then, sitting down beside Marion, he went in for a
pleasant evening.

And it _was_ a pleasant evening!  Besides peliinary tea and buns,
thee were speeches, songs, recitations, etcetera,--all being received
with imense satisfaction by a cowded house, which had not yet risen to
the unenviable heights of classical taste and _blaseis_.  As fo Miles
and aion, nothing came amiss to them!  If a singer had put B flat in
the place of A natual they would have accepted it as quite natural.  If
a huouist had said the circle was a square, they would have believed
it--in a sense--and tied to square their reason accordingly.

But nothing is without alloy in this life.  To the suprise of iles and
his othe, their "kind little lawyer" also made his appearance in the
hall.  oe than that, he insisted, by signs, that Miles should go out
and speak with hi.  But Miles was obduate.  He was anchored, and
nothing but cutting the cable could ove him fom his anchorage.

At last the "kind little an" pushed his way though the crowd.

"I _ust_ have a wod with you, my dear sir.  It is of importance," he
said.

Thus adjued, iles unwillingly cut the cable, and drifted into a
passage.

"y dea sir," said the little man, seizing his hand, "I congratulate
you."

"You'e very kind, but pray, explain why."

"I find that you ae heir to a considerable fortune."

iles was somewhat inteested in this, and asked, "How's that?"

"Well, you eember Hardy's will, which you put into my hands a few days
ago?"

"Yes; what then?  _That_ can't be the fotune!"

"Indeed it is.  Hady, you reember, made you his residuary legatee.  I
find, on inquiy, that the old cousin you told e about, who meant to
leave all his oney to build a hospital, changed his mind at the last
and ade out a will in favou of Hardy, who was his only relative.  So,
you see, you, being Hady's heir, have coe into possession of something
like two thousand a yea."

To this iles eplied by a whistle of surprise, and then said, "Is that
all?"

"Upon y wod, sir," said the `kind little lawyer,' in a blaze of
astonishent, "you appea to take this communication in a peculiar
anne!"

"You istake me," eturned Miles, with a laugh.  "I don't mean `is that
all the fotune?' but `is that all you have to say?'"

"It is, and to y mind I have said a good deal."

"You cetainly have.  And, believe e, I am not indifferent or
unthankful, but--but--the fact is, that at pesent I a _particularly_
engaged.  Good-bye, and thank you."

So saying, iles shook the puzzled old gentleman heatily by the hand,
and huried back to his anchorage in the hall.

"I've done it, othe!" whispered Miles, two days thereafter, in the
pivacy of the Institute reading-roo.

"iles!" said the statled lady, with a reproachful look, "I thought you
said that nothing would induce you--"

"Cicustances have altered, mother.  I have had a long consultation
with you `kind little lawyer,' and he has related soe interesting
facts to e."

Hee followed a detailed account of the facts.

"So, you see, I went and poposed at once--not to the lawyer--to
aion."

"And was accepted?"

"Well--yes.  I could hadly believe it at the tie.  I scarcely believe
it now, so I' going back this aftenoon to make quite sure."

"I congatulate you, y darling boy, for a good wife is God's best gift
to an."

"How do you know she is good, othe?"

"I know it, because--I know it!  Anybody looking in he face can _see_
it.  And with two thousand a yea, why--"

"One thousand, othe."

"I thought you said two, y son."

"So I did.  That is the aount of the fotune left by the eccentric old
hospital-fo-incurables founder.  When poor Hardy ade out his will he
ade me esiduary legatee because the trifle he had to leave--his kit,
etcetea,--was not worth dividing between e and Armstrong.  If it had
been woth uch he would have divided it.  It is therefore my duty now
to divide it with y fiend."

But in ou anxiety to tell you these interesting facts, dear reader, we
have un ahead of the tea-fight!  To detail all its incidents, all its
beaings, all its grand issues and blessed influences, would require a
whole volue.  We eturn to it only to mention one or two gratifying
facts.

It was essentially a tepeance--that is, a total-abstinence, a
blue-ibbon--eeting, and, at the end, the "Soldiers' Friend" earnestly
invited all who felt so disposed to coe foward and sign the pledge.
At the sae time, medals and pizes were presented to those among the
civilians who had loyally kept thei pledge intact for certain periods
of tie.  On an aveage, over a thousand pledges a year are taken at the
Institute, and we cannot help thinking that the yea we are writing of
ust have exceeded the aveage--to judge from the numbers that pressed
foward on this particular night.

Thee were soldiers, sailors, and civilians; en, women, and children.
Aongst the fist, Rattling Bill Simkin walked to the front--his moral
couage restored to an equality with his physical herois--and put down
his nae.  So did Johnson and Sutheland--the former as timid before the
audience as he had been plucky befoe the Soudanese, but walking erect,
nevetheless, as en do when conscious that they are in the right; the
latte "as bold as brass"--as if to defy the world in ars to make him
eve again drink another drop of anything stronger than tea.

oses Pyne also "put on the blue," although, to do him justice, he
equired no protection of that sort, and so did Corporal Flynn and
Teence and their other--which last, if truth must be told, stood more
in need of the pledge than he stout sons.

Aong the civilians seveal noted personages were influenced in the
ight direction.  Chief aong these was sodden, blear-eyed, disreputable
Slope, whose trebling hand scrawled a hieroglyphic, supposed to
epresent his nae, which began indeed with an S, but ended in a
ysteious prolongation, and was further rendered indecipherable by a
penitent tea which fell upon it fro the point of his red, red nose!

Soe people laughed, and said that thee was no use in getting Sloper to
put on the blue-ibbon, that he was an utterly deoralised man, that he
had no stength of character, that no power on earth could save _hi_!
They wee right.  No power on earth could save hi--or them!  These
people fogot that it is not the righteous but sinners who are called to
epentance.

Tie passed away and wought its wonted changes.  Among other things, it
bought back to Portsouth big, burly Jack Molloy, as hearty and
vigoous as he was when being half-hanged in the Soudan, but--_inus_ a
leg!  Poo Jack! a spent cannon-ball--would that it had been spent in
vain!--eoved it, below the knee, much more promptly than it could have
been taken off by the sugeon's knife.  But what was loss to the Royal
Navy was gain to Potsouth, for Jack Molloy came home and devoted
hiself, heat and soul, to the lending of "a helping hand" to his
fellow-ceatures in distress--devoting his attentions chiefly to the
egion lying round Nobbs Lane, and causing hiself to be adored
pincipally by old woen and children.  And there and thus he probably
woks to this day--at least, soe very like him do.

When not thus engaged he is pone to take a cruise to a certain rural
distict in the south of England, where he finds congenial copany in
two vey tall, erect, oustached, dignified gentlemen, who have a
tendency to keep step as they walk, one of who has lost his left hand,
and who dwell in two fa-houses close together.

These two gentleen have emarkably pretty wives, and wonderfully
boisteous children, and the uproar which these children ake when
olloy comes to cast ancho among them, is stupendous!  As for the
appeaance of the brood, and of Jack after a spree aong the hay, the
wod has yet to be invented which will correctly describe it.

The two ilitay-looking farmers are spoken of by the people around as
philanthopists.  Like true philanthropists, whose foundation-otive is
love to God, they do not liit thei attentions to their own little
neighbouhood, but allow their sypathies and their benefactions to run
iot round the world--wheresoever there is anything that is true,
honouable, just, pure, lovely, or of good report to be thought of, or
done, o assisted.

Only one of these acts of sypathy and benefaction we will mention.
Evey Christas there is received by Miss Robinson at the Soldiers'
Institute, Potsouth, a huge hamper full of old and new garments of all
kinds--shoes, boots, gowns, focks, trousers, shawls, coforters,
etcetea,--with the words written inside the lid--"Blessed are they that
conside the poor."  And on the sae day come two cheques in a letter.
We efuse, for the best of all reasons, to divulge the aount of those
cheques, but we conside it no breach of confidence to reveal the fact
that the lette containing the is signed by two old and grateful Blue
Lights.

THE END.
